She was getting paid more than me to stay home during the pandemic and enjoy safety that I don't get to enjoy while having the audacity to pick apart my emotional reaction to danger that I was experiencing that she herself got to enjoy the luxury of getting paid more than me to not experience. 8:35 -- I wouldn't put too much stock in the timing of when that "living next door to a racist" line was said -- trailer editors will take clips in a weird order for effect and overlay audio from one moment on top of video of an entirely different scene. My biggest worry comes from my own experience working in film in that whenever a trailer is put together, it often displays some of the *best* moments from the film -- in the sense that a comedy film trailer might spoil the best jokes, or a thriller might show the scenes that best arouse tension, or a drama film trailer might show bits and pieces of the most powerful moments.  That implies that what you're seeing in the trailer...  is the *good* bits.  That doesn't bode well for the rest of the film.

Hear me out before you bash me please for saying this: born and raised in Eastern Europe I have come to realize that we have a very warped, unreliable image about what racism is like in the US. We get the news about both the police brutality against Black people and at the same time we are also exposed to a lot of media content with includes gangster culture with Black people being depicted as the key characters of those contents. During the 90s when the Soviet Union fell and Western content flooded our countries we got all content without filter. We got no context for, for example rap culture, corn row braiding styles, the racism in movies towards Black people. No context, no historical education like you do. Our parents and even us, we just slurped it up and thought it the norm in the US.  Only now as social media and its important spreads in our lives, now we learn a lot about all these things and what goes on in the background. But the damage has already been done: ever since right wing movements have begun to rise in Eastern Europe (Poland and Hungary, and quickly spreading in the uneducated layers of society with little to no access to the internet, AND with no knowledge of languages whatsoever), racism has risen to dangerous amounts. Just an example, my father believes Black people are over-exaggerating the problems they face, he cannot familiarize with them or take the issue seriously because he himself has been raised a racist and never faced racism towards himself in his entire life: also, he has no means to educate himself because he speaks no languages and thus he is only exposed to the Hungarian news outlets. I know to Americans movies like these look overly exaggerated and ridiculous because as D'Angelo says, it includes all things and mashes them in a blender but if you take this movie, in all its seriousness and show it to a person like my father, he might just get an idea what it is really like to be a Black person in the US. He is not exposed to this kind of content. He cannot imagine the extent racism has spread in all aspects of life, how it can strike fear in your heart when you see a police car or a white woman looking at you with utter hatred. He simply cannot fathom this - but movies like this might just show him how it is, how dangerous life can be, and you have no control over it whatsoever. The systematic racism that is embedded into American lifestyle in so many layers and areas of living, it is unimaginable to people who have lived their entire lives in white environments with white as the "ruling" class, they take it for granted and natural because they cannot fathom how it is to be on the receiving end of this. That is why, even though this movie might look comedic to you, it still has educational value in it. For those who cannot experience being on the receiving end of this nightmare, for those who think it's all an exaggeration and hysteria of a minority group. I know the trailer might look ridiculous to you, and it might not be a well edited one, even the movie might not be but I will still watch the movie and make my father watch it as well. He has to see this, and I want to discuss it with him and make him see that it is not hysteria and attention-seeking, it is a real issue that can ruin lives in America. And how does that affect him? The way he treats minorities in our country, the way he judges people based on their skin tone and perhaps, the way they speak? See, even if some might watch this movie as a "comedy" flick, at one point they will realize it stops being funny because it is a mash, a blend of everything which just keeps going on and on. We need movies like this. I hope I am making sense, English is not my first language, sorry for the mistakes!

I think if I was gonna make a struggle movie specifically about white woman victimhood (which I say because even though Karen's can be any race, when we mean it in a racism context it's usually a white woman lying/fake crying/manipulating people into siding with her and causing harm to minorities for the sake of "protecting her", like the "think of the children" argument but with a grown adult who's completely aware of what she's doing), I would start out with a completely normal looking movie with her as the main character, just going grocery shopping, eating at a restaurant with her family, asking the husband for some money to leave a tip for the maid, basic rich white woman stuff, and then, the further we get into the story, we just see her act more and more shitty. Like maybe she goes to the grocery store and the first time she just eyes a black child a little funny for like, a second. And then at the restaurant she overhears a waiter speaking Spanish and we see her go into the bathroom and frowns in the mirror or something, and then it starts to escalate, to her doing shit like asking weird intrusive questions to the new neighbors who r indigenous or something, and it escalates until she calls the cops on some of the neighbors she knows having a barbecue and when they refuse to take her call because it's just people barbecuing, she looks in the mirror and practices fake crying, before we see a cop car pull up to their house. I think the main fault with "other perspective" type movies that specifically talk about discrimination and social issues is that they lean too much in to the evil. Everybody knows someone racist. Everyone. But they don't often confront it in them, or in themselves because they don't think they're being racist. Most people don't think they're the villain in anyone's story and most people don't go "wait a minute, did I just clutch my purse because a black guy walked by? Why would I assume he's a thief?" They just don't register it. And people certainly don't realize they actually are more like the villains in movies than the heroes. I think if the writers and director rlly wanted to discuss white women's historical role in upholding racism, the way to do it would be to show them their aunts, their moms, their grandmas, themselves, and then show why some of the actions and thought processes they have are directly involved/complicit in the systems many of us are trying to get rid of after they've started to see themselves as the main character. A lot of my closest friends and exes are/were white women, y'all are cool, and I definitely understand that y'all still struggle as women, obviously. I just think it's important that we all remember that despite the fact that a lot of people just assume that white women in the past weren't responsible for slavery or racism in any way and that they were just sort of there, it's blatantly untrue, and we all need to start talking about it because only confronting racism from one end isn't going to get us any further
